### Post by Lovechild on 2005-11-11
[QUOTE=Anthem]I've stayed out of this thread for a week, trying not to bump it.  It just won't die.

Lovechild et al., answer me this.  Let's say we ditch firefox and use Epiphany.  How long would it be before you started to gripe about OpenOffice?[/QUOTE]

Oh I already gripe about OpenOffice, I think it's slow, bloated, ugly and suffers from a case of bad design - however it is the best Office suite we have currently. I elect to use GNOME Office because I like Abiword better (it does have some technical advantages like grammar checking - english only atm though). This does not change the fact that GNOME Office isn't complete, there is no presenter and it lacks a lot compared to OpenOffice.

The default installation should probably stay OpenOffice, even though it's my assessment that the codebase will need at least another 3-5 years of reworking before it's really good - it basically suffers from the same problem Mozilla did when we got it, it's badly designed, cluttered and breaking it into modules will take time. It does seem to be the case with any large chunk of code we get from corporations, while a nice gesture it's a lot of work to salvage.

I do believe that GNOME Office (or KOffice if that's your flavor - KOffice has some interesting ideas to be honest) is the right way to do things, but currently they don't offer a similar featureset and at least Abiword isn't on l10n-status either so that application is not fully translated into Danish currently (whereas the OpenOffice translation while inconsistent at least is rather complete). I'm very impressed with the progress in the Abiword camp though and I do recommend it to people who just need a wordprocessor.

So don't worry, OpenOffice wins this one quite nicely.

Now on to answering some Epiphany questions:

Adblock is adjustable with perl regexp, which should give some hint as to it's power beneath the bonnet. I am unsure as to the UI for this I have to admit I haven't played with it in quite a while - I'm hesitant to compile it myself so I'm waiting for it to hit Dapper for further testing and UI review.

Maximizing screen space, you mean editing your toolbars I think, Edit->Toolbars - you can remove the text under the icons, adjust the icons amount and drag items around to suit you. On my setup with text under the icons I use roughly the same amount of pixels as Firefox' default and I have a little smart bookmark to search google (or whichever engine you happen to like).

Themes, well the point is to have Epiphany look like the rest of the desktop - so themes are handled by GNOME, want another one replace the GNOME theme - I quite like Human widgets, Clearlooks window decorations and Tango icons.

My Epiphany defaults to presenting me with an option to open all bookmarks in a folder - however in 1.9.1 they seem to have reworked the way bookmarks work and they now seem to work more like Places does in Nautilus - it seems a little strange to me, I'm not sure I like it (having only played with it for a couple of minutes).

Bloglines notifier, I am unsure what this does - I assume is an RSS client, in which case it has no purpose in a webbrowser, seperating it out as is done by Epiphany seems the right choice designwise. I assume this could work, but now we are getting into fairly specific functionality aren't we?

---

### Post by Limulus on 2005-11-11
[QUOTE=The Warlock]The type of people that are most likely to consider Linux (and Ubuntu is rapidly becoming the most popular distribution for starting out on Linux) are the people that are already somewhat computer-savvy, and in these circles, Firefox is much more common than IE. The kind of person that knows their computer, at least sort of, will be more swayed by "look, some of those really nice programs you like are here, too" instead of "hey, you need to learn everything all over again!"[/QUOTE]

I'm going to add my $0.02 to this discussion.

First of all, *The Warlock* is exactly right :)  I can say that because the above quote is in fact an eerily accurate description of myself ;)

I was sick and tired of being all paranoid about malware and so started using Firefox at version 0.8  Gradually I discovered the most magical thing about Firefox: extensions.

Imagine being served a small bowl of plain vanilla ice cream.  That is the average browser (some slightly fancier ones are french vanilla... IE is the cheap artificially flavored stuff that comes in half gallon tubs for $3 ^_-).  Firefox extensions are sprinkles and fudge and butterscotch and fruit and candy bits and anything else you can imagine.  You want mint flavored ice cream?  Easy.  You want *durian* flavored ice cream?  There's a good chance someone's already thought of that.

Now, the average Firefox user will probably have found a couple extensions.  The average power user probably a few dozen.  I have used the Cards extension to help get people away from IE to FF :)

Windows, to continue our ice cream analogy, is the table at which you sit to eat your bowl of ice cream.  Ubuntu is a different (better ;-)) table.  But with Firefox, the experience is otherwise basically the same.

Now, you put a Windows Firefox user (who has personalized Firefox to their tastes) in an environment with some other browser and either they will either say 'no thanks, I like what I have now' or they'll ask 'will it run Firefox?'  Either way, it negates the point of making the other browser the default.

If you want to increase people's interest in other browsers, why not start an Ubuntu tutorial (maybe even have it as part of the default Ubuntu install) that shows all the different browsers available to them? (in addition to other program choices).  See, the thing is that Epiphany *looks* a tad dull and my gut response would be to switch to Opera or Konqueror if I was somehow forced to give up Firefox because I'd actually *heard* about them before ;)  I think you'd even get more interest in potential converts from Windows if you offered *Flock* instead of Epiphany.

### Post by Cl1mh4224rd on 2005-11-11
Ok, as a relative Linux/Ubuntu newbie, I have to say this...

**GNOME integration/consistency is *not* as big of a deal as some of you *want* it to be.**

Some of you make it sound like Firefox sticks out like a sore thumb, and it just doesn't. There is no newbie who is going to be poking around Ubuntu, checking out apps, then get to Firefox and go, "Wtf?"

It's just not going to happen.

Edit:

I'd also like to point out that Firefox can be just as "simple" to use for those who don't want to do anything fancy. If someone just wants to browse the web, the back, forward, refresh, and stop buttons, along with the address bar are still there, and that's all a newbie really needs. Having the extra, hardly "advanced", features available for those who go digging around is *not* going to hurt.

### Post by aysiu on 2005-11-13
[QUOTE=idn]Its a very handy feature, and its another example of how firefox is inferior due to the many small features it lacks which epiphany has[/quote] You're joking about this, too, surely. Have you read this thread? You cannot name a single feature Epiphany has that Firefox cannot add through an extension such as, say, [All-in-One Gestures](https://addons.mozilla.org/extensions/moreinfo.php?id=12) (see attached screenshot). I can, however, as I've done in [other posts](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=482223&postcount=183), name plenty of features Firefox has that Epiphany doesn't. 

For me, it's really important that I can open a bunch of tabs from a particular bookmarks folder all at once. I also like having a Bloglines notifier in my browser, advanced (and easily editable) ad blocking, selective Javascript blocking, [advanced cookie handling](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=89635), and multiple search engines (whichever ones I want--Google Maps, Wikipedia, Amazon, whatever) available in my browser, and in Epiphany it's either 1. not easily apparent how this is possible or 2. just not possible.

Also, recently, someone was complaining about not being able to use OldNavy.com. In Firefox, you just need the User Agent Switcher extension to pretend you're Internet Explorer. What do you do in Epiphany?

And that's why this thread keeps going round and round. People are pretending it's a "which browser should be the default for Dapper" thread when it's really a "which browser is best?" thread.

### Post by aysiu on 2005-11-13
In an argument about what's more important in a **default** install, I'd say the new user who knows nothing about Gnome and Gnome integration and typical Gnome behavior.

The vast majority of Ubuntu users who came from Windows used Firefox before coming over, and it's the one point of comfort in a sea of unfamiliar programs (GIMP, Rhythmbox, Evolution, etc.). Firefox is a capable browser, and the only objections I've heard to it are how it looks (which doesn't matter to a new user, especially since it can be themed) and how it behaves with tabs and the scroll wheel (which is a semi-obscure behavior *and* can be solved with a Firefox extension).

Anyone familiar enough with "typical" Gnome-based application behaviors will have no problems typing ```
sudo apt-get install epiphany-browser
``` in a terminal.

You can disagree if you'd like, but I think making Ubuntu accessible to Windows migrants is a worthy goal. That's why there's only one application per task and they're labeled by the task, not the program (Rhythmbox music player, not just Rhythmbox).

Ubuntu is designed for new users--not for Gnome veterans. Gnome veterans can tweak all they want.

---

### Post by Lovechild on 2005-11-13
[QUOTE=Curlydave]What's so great about it being integrated? Who cares? What about the basic ability native to every desktop environment/OS that's existed in modern times that has an option to change the rate of wheel scrolling? Epiphany/Gnome needs to integrate by including this option which is IMO, common-sense.[/QUOTE]

Here's a nice benefit from being integrated (as a whole - not epiphany specifically)

- Less code to support
Nobody implements their own version of some brainless common method - there's one standard and at least then things break only one place.

- Better tested general cases
Since everyone now walks the same datapath, the chance of finding all critical bugs fast is increased. It also increases the chances of hitting all the cornercases that nobody thinks off, till some random bloke is able to reproduce a bug.

- Centralisation of information
We don't store the same information multiple times, it also enables sharing information between programs easier (such as integrating your IM buddy list with the addressebook in you mail client so to make a central contactlist)

- Improved work efficency 
Since the inferface works like the rest of the platform you don't have to learn many out of the norm tasks with each added program and since we share information across the platform you can access it when you need thus saving you time from entering it multople times.

- Better software
Since software now is safer, faster and more bugfree than ever before and to boot you are working more efficently, you are happy and can complain about unrelated socio-political matters instead of your computer.

---

### Post by doclivingston on 2005-11-14
[QUOTE=hoodwink]A side thought. Shuttleworth has now stated that Kubuntu will be supported as a co-equal. Does it make sense to have a default browser that is Gnome dependant? Switching Kubuntu's default browser to Firefox would cause a much bigger debate than this one is, because Firefox is much less integrated into KDE than it is into Gnome. ### Post by aysiu on 2005-11-14
Considering the case of Kubuntu reinforces how ridiculous this whole debate is. As I've stated before (even though I'm inclined toward keeping Firefox as the default), it *really* doesn't matter, as can be seen with Kubuntu. If the native browser is the default...

1. Users who don't care will use whatever's presented to them
2. Users who care will install whatever browser they want (sudo apt-get install firefox)

If Firefox is the default browser...

1. Users who don't care will use whatever's presented to them
2. Users who care will use Epiphany or Konqueror (Konqueror will be there regardless...)

Is using GTK themes a feature? I don't want to spend hours scouring the web to find a firefox theme that matches every other application I use.

> [All-in-One Gestures](https://addons.mozilla.org/extensions/moreinfo.php?id=12) (see attached screenshot). I can, however, as I've done in [other posts](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=482223&postcount=183), name plenty of features Firefox has that Epiphany doesn't. 

Epiphany has a gestures extension.


> For me, it's really important that I can open a bunch of tabs from a particular bookmarks folder all at once. I also like having a Bloglines notifier in my browser, advanced (and easily editable) ad blocking, selective Javascript blocking, [advanced cookie handling](http://www.ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=89635), and multiple search engines (whichever ones I want--Google Maps, Wikipedia, Amazon, whatever) available in my browser, and in Epiphany it's either 1. not easily apparent how this is possible or 2. just not possible.

I wouldn't say that users who just use the default browser, and aren't going to install their favourite, are likely to use things like selective cookie and javascript blocking. Epiphany supports multiple search engines, and I have "Open in New Tabs" in my topic's drop-down on the bookmark bar.


> Also, recently, someone was complaining about not being able to use OldNavy.com. In Firefox, you just need the User Agent Switcher extension to pretend you're Internet Explorer. What do you do in Epiphany?

Use Epiphany's User Agent Switcher extension.


> And that's why this thread keeps going round and round. People are pretending it's a "which browser should be the default for Dapper" thread when it's really a "which browser is best?" thread.

"which browser should be the default for Dapper" is basically "which browser is best for users who aren't going to install their favourite anyway"

The default has to be Firefox, people who come from Windows or Mac are incredibly likely to have used Firefox and will most likely be happy that there is less crap to install. Generally speaking default settings should be the most popular ones, which means Firefox. Additionally like someone else said before "if it ain't broke don't fix it".

### Post by alex-weej on 2005-11-20
Epiphany is excellent. There is no replacement for a native GTK+ UI. I'm sorry but XUL just doesn't cut it - it may be close visually, but there is more to a UI than meets the eye (I'm talking subtle features like being able to scroll through tabs, etc.).

My argument towards switching:

1. session saving capability
2. native UI
3. more integrated with GNOME than Fx will ever be
4. ephy extensions are actually /really/ good - the Page Info extension is much better than Fx's because it allows drag and drop from, for example, the media tab.
5. the Galeon squad seems to have disbanded with the intention of bringing all of Galeon's functionality into Ephy through extension. Hurrah!

My mum had a bit of a shit fit the other day when she was getting confused between OOo "open" dialogue, Fx "save" dialogue, Nautilus and GtkFileChooser.

Now considering these are all standard Ubuntu apps, shouldn't they be a *little* more polished? :confused:

Won't someone think of Grandma?! :P

Epiphany

[SIZE="7"]**EPIPHANY**[/SIZE]

### Post by Foaming Draught on 2005-11-20
There is a saying in English, "The best is the enemy of the good". It means that in chasing an elusive "best", or wasting time agreeing what is best, perfectly good things get crowded out and mediocrity wins.
For a variety of reasons, I prefer Epiphany to Firefox. Other folk will prefer Opera. But we're not concerned to get this or that browser onto the world's desktops, we're passionate about GNU/Linux in general and ubuntu in particular. It seems to me that Dapper developers are picking a default browser by asking the question, "What's best for ubuntu's exponential take-up?", and I have to agree with them that the answer is Firefox.
I'll still default to Epiphany for so long as it represents a perceived edge for Gnome users (and even that's subjective, I mean for so long as it represents a perceived edge for me), but out-of-the-box should be Firefox.

### Post by JoWilly on 2005-11-21
[QUOTE=MadMan2k]
2) Epiphany will be always faster than Firefox since it builds upon the latest firefox, but uses GTK instead of XUL for the Interface
[/QUOTE]

No, not on my system (P4@3.2ghz, Dapper).

I am using firefox, but seeing all the noise about this on these forums I tried epiphany again 2 days ago.

Result:
[LIST]
[*]**Firefox starts much faster than epiphany (and firefox 1.5 is even faster)**. I was surprised to see than epiphany was slower to start, I was expecting different from a "gnome" app.
[*]Firefox is more complete.
[*]Firefox also uses gnome open/save dialog, widgets,etc... -> is well integrated.
[*]Epihany is not better where it could (and should) : the html form widgets are the same as firefox (although there are patches arround to improve firefox, epiphany as a gome application cannot even display gnome widgets).
[*]Epiphany is beeing developed at a VERY slow pace... I have tried it several times during the past 2 years to check on it, and it is still ugly today (see the bookmarks manager, etc...)
[*] New web technology appears in firefox first; and as epiphany development is so slow...
[/LIST]

**To them, they don't care about "new web technology" - but they don't want to be told off for running Firefox when you try to log out with the "save session" checkbox ticked.**

### Post by Jengu on 2005-11-21
I can't say I've read the whole thread -- but keep in mind that if you're talking about dapper you should be comparing epiphany against Firefox 1.5, which will be stable long before dapper ships. Firefox is more widely tested for security vulnerabilities due to its popularity, has a popular community of extension developers, will be familiar to many windows geeks, and doesn't strike me as easier or harder to use than Epiphany. It's been awhile since I used it, but last I checked it was mostly the gecko renderer with back/forward buttons.

---

### Post by doclivingston on 2005-11-21
[QUOTE=Jengu]I can't say I've read the whole thread -- but keep in mind that if you're talking about dapper you should be comparing epiphany against Firefox 1.5, which will be stable long before dapper ships.[/quote]

For the same reason you also need to make the comparisons against Epiphany 1.10 (1.9.x for now).

> Firefox is more widely tested for security vulnerabilities due to its popularity

Almost all the security vulnerabilities are Gecko not Firefox problems, so will be fixed in Epiphany at the same time as they are fixed in Firefox.


[quote=Benjamin_Lebsanft]epiphany doesn't focus the location bar when opening a new tab[/quote]

This is a GtkEmbedMoz bug, and affects *everything* using gecko, apart from Firefox/Mozilla.


[quote=gadren]This poll would seem to suggest that most Ubuntu users user Firefox before moving to Ubuntu.[/quote]

Yes, but that means what exactly? Most Epiphany users are former firefox users, and most of them use Firefox when they are on a windows machine.

[quote=Zotova]Another problem with epiphany is when I downloaded Firefox 1.5 guess what... epiphany stopped working... So it got deleted. If epiphany is default then it needs to work whatever happens as there will be a lot of complaints if people like to test out Firefox nightly builds and then all of a sudden epiphany doesn't work.[/quote]

If you uninstalled the old version of Firefox then it won't work. That's because Epiphany depends on the version of Gecko it was built against (until XULRunner is stable). The same will happen when you upgrade any package that breaks API/ABI.

You can do all those things in Epiphany - please do some research before posting such uninformed rants, or take the time to read the thread. 

but in your honor I'll repeat the information I have posted earlier in this thread:

Theme, the point of using an desktop is that all applications share the same basic characteristics, such as widget theme - I tend to favor clearlooks-cairo (gtk-engine-2.7.0 and beyond).
Gestures, in Epiphany Extensions - should they not forfill your needs unlike with the number of firefox extensions, it's very easy to report a bug on these as they are in the gnome bugzilla.
Tab adaption, also in the extensions package - haven't played with this as I don't really need it - but it does the needed mingling around with tabs, move them around, seperate them out, etc.
Ad blocking, also in extensions (disabled by default in Ubuntu right now I think - but this does work and will work within dapper timeframe)
Toolbars, edit->toolbars to hard to discover.. and wow if you add a smart bookmark like the one found in the bookmark toolbar your google (or which ever engine you like) search is present (I do think this needs to be the default but I haven't mocked up a patch to do this yet).

By my count you are now down to zero arguments for Firefox LinuxSwede.

### Post by Lovechild on 2005-11-22
"Can I configure every aspect of the operation", no you can't that is the point of GNOME - good defaults - now apply that thinking to your DE, please try out KDE, you can do all sorts of stuff that mostly applies only to a marginal number of users. Just like Firefox and it's many extensions much of what is described as advantages will only apply to less than 1% of all users, while piling on options and all sorts of poweruser features.

Please don't obstruct the quest to hit the greatest common benefactor with elitist poweruser arguments.

If you are a poweruser and truly cannot live with something like being able to exactly set the given actions for gestures or determining just how tabs should open - you can do sudo apt-get install firefox.

### Post by reinouts on 2006-02-28
[QUOTE=fangorious]The only three things that would make me consider Epiphany over Firefox are gnome-keyring integration, the ability to create searchable PDF documents with a print driver, and a comparable set of extensions.[/QUOTE]
GNOME keyring integration is not there yet, but for those who would like to help, please refer to the [Epiphany wiki](http://live.gnome.org/Epiphany/FeatureDesign/GnomeKeyring). With regard to PDF documents; this is completely dependent on gnome-print. Finally, Epiphany will never be able to match the sheer number of Firefox extensions, but there are quite a few good and useful ones available already. You can even write them in Python or Mono/C# if you like.

---

### Post by reinouts on 2006-02-28
[QUOTE=stodge]the bookmarks are too simplistic[/QUOTE]
You obviously haven't used Epiphany 1.9.x; the bookmarks have been given a complete overhaul. The bookmarks menu now automagically creates topic-based groupings and subtopics for you.

Regardless, epiphany bookmarks never were simplistic - they had topics before del.icio.us tagging became popular - but you shouldn't try to use the bookmarks menu like a folder tree. Instead, type topics or words from the page title in the address bar and the autocomplete will provide all relevant matches.

---

### Post by paulle on 2006-02-28

---

### Post by doclivingston on 2006-02-28
[QUOTE=VitaminG]Why does anyone want a web browser that's fully integrates into the desktop environment? One of the things I hated most about IE was the fact that you CANNOT uninstall it. Uninstalling IE made explorer crash, which meant any program that saves files wouldn't work properly. Soon you have a machine that's only good for playing Minesweeper and Solitaire. I have'nt used Epiphany, but I have loved Firefox since 1.0preview on Windows, before I switched to Linux. And the best improvement an OS can make, IMHO, is to make it as modular as possible, and not integrate everything.[/QUOTE]

"Integrated" here means something different. Epiphany is "integrated" with the Gnome desktop, because it uses the services that the Gnome desktop has to offer:

* Epiphany doesn't have a proxy configuration dialog, it uses the gnome settings.
* Epiphany uses the standard GTK theme
* Epiphany's toolbars follow the desktop-wide setting for icons/text
* Epiphany doesn't ask you what application you want to open things with, it uses the gnome-wide settings
* Epiphany uses your preferred text editor to look at page source.
* and so on...

---

### Post by reinouts on 2006-02-28
[QUOTE=fangorious]but would like to be able to change it, at least be able to say "ignore global proxy settings" to get no proxy.[/quote]
Sounds like a nice candidate for an extension :p (Should be quite simple, actually, all we need is a menu item to toggle a gconf key). But for the basic UI, we don't want to pollute it with settings that the vast majority of users doesn't know or care about.
> An error window pops up saying printing to PDF is not supported. See the screenshot. 
Oops, you are correct, that's [bug 164835](http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=164385). :-k But I was correct too in that it is dependent on GNOME-print. It needs to work with cairo.
> I suppose I could try and port some extensions from Firefox to Epiphany. Would at least give some recent development experience to put on my resume.
That would be great! :cool: As a starting point, you can read the [Epiphany extensions tutorial](http://www.gnome.org/projects/epiphany/documentation/extensions/).

### Post by polpak on 2006-02-28
[QUOTE=teleon]I disagree.  The majority of users who would consider switching to ubuntu are very likely to already have switched to an alternative brower.[/QUOTE]

The polls certainly do show this as being a trend.

That being said, I do agree with advantages the Epiphany voters are touting. Integration is a good thing, but it's not the only thing. There are several things I see as standing in the way of making Epiphany the default. 

Firstly it's Epiphany's dependance on Firefox. Yes this will change, and when it does this issue can certainly be reopened. But until then this is a pretty big sticking point. Why have Epiphany + Firefox when you can just have Firefox. Particularly in light of the next point.

Firefox is more feature rich. There are a ton of extentions, and there are a lot of people using them. Even beyond that there are plenty of plain old usibility features lacking in Epiphany (see previous posts about cookie management, etc.) Again, this is changing and I'm sure that Epiphany will close this gap. But it's just not there yet.

There is also the issue of new users transitioning to linux and wanting to provide them with familiar apps. I do see this as a point in favor of Firefox, but if the first two issues were addressed, this would hardly be enough of an issue, in my mind, to warrent keeping Firefox over Epiphany.

One of the greatest strengths of Ubuntu is that most things "Just Work" and for me at least Epiphany doesn't ... yet.

### Post by reinouts on 2006-03-01
[QUOTE=stodge]Oh and I don't think Epiphany lets you create sub-folders for bookmarks.[/QUOTE]
Epiphany does not work with the concept of bookmarks (sub-)folders at all, but rather with topics. The current development version creates an automatic topic "hierarchy" for you in the Bookmarks menu based on overlapping topic selections. If you want to know more about this, there's a [long explanation](http://peter.a.harvey.googlepages.com/epiphany) available.

---

### Post by RS3York on 2006-03-04
I think that the default browser should pass the Acid2 test :p

[QUOTE=aysiu]
So to all the "Epiphany has better integration" folks, where's your thread about AbiWord and Gnumeric v. OpenOffice?
[/QUOTE]

Game, Set, Match - aysiu.

More seriously though, this is a good point.  As are the prior points about 'mindshare'.  I've read a few times that "mindshare is the only currency in the opensource community", this makes sense.  I hear the whole "integration" argument.  But I think that in certain cases integration can be and *is* overlooked for greater goals.  Sure, one would want integration *most* of the time, but I know that integration doesn't stop people from installing Gimp on their WinXP installations.  As far as defaults go, the most popular should reign. Firefox has more mindshare, more functionality, a more familiar interface and currently needs to be installed for Epiphany to work.  It would be more efficient to use Firefox instead of adding packages.


[QUOTE=ow50]If you're implying that there's some lack of consistency, I assure you, that is not the case. The case of Epiphany and Firefox is just much clearer as Epiphany is a complete replacement for Firefox. OOo is quite a piece of junk as well and does not fit in GNOME, but the difference is that AbiWord and Gnumeric or even the whole gnome-office metapackage do not completely replace OOo. Most importantly there's no GNOME application to replace OOo Impress.[/QUOTE]

Well Epiphany may be a complete replacement in its *function* (assuming you aren't using any niche extensions) it's really an addition concerning the install CD.

You may feel that OOo is a piece of junk, but in the linux desktop space many people can more easily replace the entire Gnome DE than OpenOffice.org!  That's pretty valuable "junk" if you ask me.  Forget integration, OpenOffice.org is an opensource cornerstone.  Many people get more comfortable trying/using/switching to a linux distro from Windows because they've tried Firefox and OpenOffice (maybe Thunderbird, Gimp & Gaim or others along the way).

I'm pretty confident you can install OOo Impress without OOo Writer, Calc, Base and so on.  There's nothing stopping a user from having Abiword, Gnumeric, Impress as their "office suite"...but as long as we're talking about defaults it should be OpenOffice.org2 for the added functionality it provides along with the "benefit of familiarity".  Just like Firefox.

As for Kubuntu, Firefox *could* be included there too.  Although the situation is different as Konqueror is also the file browser.  So the Kubuntu Team saves on a package by leaving Firefox out.  I suspect that firefox might be included in the future for the sake of consistency & familiarity, given Mark's comments about Kubuntu.

### Post by aysiu on 2006-03-05
[QUOTE=Segovia]
Firefox doesn't look right in gnome OR KDE and it's a turtle.  Slowest browser I've ever used in my life.[/QUOTE] Really? I just compared the two, and they ran at the same speed. I clicked "New Posts" in Ubuntu Forums in Epiphany, and it took 3 seconds to load. I clicked "New Posts" in Ubuntu Forums in Firefox, and it took 3 seconds to load.

I don't see what's so overrated about Firefox. I can open all my bookmarks in a particular folder by middle-clicking the folder or selecting "open in tabs." I can clear all personal data with one click. I have sophisticated cookie management. I have a search menu with drop-downs to the Ubuntu Packages site, Amazon, IMDB, the dictionary, Google Maps, and just about anything else I want to add. I have a Bloglines notifier. My bookmarks toolbar can be dragged to the top toolbar to save space. The tabs can cycle around (from the last tab to the first tab) with keyboard shortcuts. This is not counting all the amazing extensions Firefox has (NoScript, SessionSaver, User Agent Switcher).

Epiphany is a minimalist browser, just above Dillo in terms of functionality. It has simplicity and integration (which, I think, is overrated), but that's about it.

### Post by tman_ubuntu on 2006-03-17
The only issues I have with Epiphany is that
- for some reason it doesn't open PDF documents within the browser (plugin doesn't work).  

-There is no place for you to change what file types you would like to use with a default application.

-No setting for bringing new tabs to the front instead of loading them in the back when clicking links.

-Popup manager is pretty featureless.  Can't find where you turn on or off individual sites.

-Non intuitive or mozilla standard way of installing/removing plugins or extensions.

-URL navigation does not have a drop down to choose recently browsed sites stored in history.  You have to type something that happens to have the same start of letters for the history you are looking for to show.

Given the fact that Epiphany is integrated with GNOME look and feel, I would much rather use it.  It seems very quick and simple.  However, I still need to have control over some of the mentioned features to go away from Firefox.

### Post by The Warlock on 2006-03-17
I've been using Ephiphany for some time now, and I'm thinking of going back to Firefox. Epiphany is indeed many times faster and integrated into GNOME, and I'll miss that. But it's got some problems. Listed in order of severity (for me, anyway):

1) GTK's default tab behavior is **terrible.** If I've got more than five or six tabs open, any extras vanish off to the side and I need to use these *tiny* arrow buttons to scroll left and right between them. I get headshots in Counter-Strike easier than I can click those damn arrows. And worse, I can't see how many tabs I have open or what's in them! Look, Firefox has them shrink for a *reason*. Don't "fix" what isn't broken. Yes, shrinking the tabs makes it hard to read what they say, but that's what that icon is for. At least have it as an option somewhere.

(Yes, I'm aware that that holds true for any other tab-using GTK2 app. That is why I have the same complaints about gedit, gaim, and others that do the same thing.)

2) The ad-blocking capabilities are inferior to Firefox's. I have gotten pop-ups that would never sneak through Firefox, even when Epiphany's popup blocker is unchecked. The adblock plugin for epiphany comes with filterset.G by default, which is good, but I can't add anything to it when I find something that sneaks through, and that's bad.

3) Typing something that's not a URL into the address bar brings up a google.co.uk search, and there is no way to change this to whatever search engine that I prefer. I've tried changing the "Search the Web" bookmark, and that changes the search engine for when I use the "search the web" option, but not just for throwing garbage into the address bar, which still uses UK google. I don't live in the UK, people. 

4) I can't see image properties when I right-click on an image. There's just no "Properties" option in the menu. I kind of liked having that there, for when I wanted to know the images size or resolution or whatever. Or for seeing the URL without copying it to the clipboard and pasting it somewhere.

5) When you add a "quick bookmark" to the toolbar, there is no way to change or remove the icon, remove the text, or really to customise it in any way.

6) The tab bar is not shown when only one tab is open, and there is nothing in the preferences box to change this. This is fixable in gconf, so it's not that serious, but I *hate* having to dive into gconf and consider it a usability failure if I find myself with no other option.

7) It would be nice if there was an easy way to get Evince to display a PDF in a tab in ephiphany, but this is not really a nessessity. It would just be nice.

---

I get headshots in Counter-Strike easier than I can click those damn arrows. And worse, I can't see how many tabs I have open or what's in them! Look, Firefox has them shrink for a *reason*. Don't "fix" what isn't broken. Yes, shrinking the tabs makes it hard to read what they say, but that's what that icon is for. At least have it as an option somewhere.

(Yes, I'm aware that that holds true for any other tab-using GTK2 app. That is why I have the same complaints about gedit, gaim, 
and others that do the same thing.)[/QUOTE]
That is *the* one big major blocker for me to even consider using Epiphany (and other apps) on a day-to-day basis. There's more missing, but without that one feature it's moot anyways.

It's scary, it's impossible to get good overview and workflow, and for some reason it prioritizes being able to close tabs (but still scroll is more important than middle-click close) over using them.

I even devoted my sig to this (rant + bugs filed), that's how strongly I feel about it.

[QUOTE=The Warlock]2) The ad-blocking capabilities are inferior to Firefox's. I have gotten pop-ups that would never sneak through Firefox, even when Epiphany's popup blocker is unchecked. The adblock plugin for epiphany comes with filterset.G by default, which is good, but I can't add anything to it when I find something that sneaks through, and that's bad.[/QUOTE]

Not to mention that a huge part of the web is ad-financed, and that many times I may be interested in seeing properly targetted, discrete ads that is related to my interest on a site I support. While it's more work the way Firefox does it, at least I can let through the ones I like, too. There are many sites I'd like to stay. 

The bad thing here is that I can't block the bad offenders and keep the good. If you need the predefined filter list, Adblock can use the same list as Epiphany uses and then you could unblcok sites (and add new ones).

[QUOTE=The Warlock]3) Typing something that's not a URL into the address bar brings up a google.co.uk search, and there is no way to change this to whatever search engine that I prefer. I've tried changing the "Search the Web" bookmark, and that changes the search engine for when I use the "search the web" option, but not just for throwing garbage into the address bar, which still uses UK google. I don't live in the UK, people.[/QUOTE]
Still better than the alternative, when they wanted to remove it altogether, because "anyone can scroll down to the search engine instead", which would have been a nother major usage blocker.

[QUOTE=The Warlock]4) I can't see image properties when I right-click on an image. There's just no "Properties" option in the menu. I kind of liked having that there, for when I wanted to know the images size or resolution or whatever. Or for seeing the URL without copying it to the clipboard and pasting it somewhere.[/QUOTE]
Agreed.

[QUOTE=The Warlock]7) It would be nice if there was an easy way to get Evince to display a PDF in a tab in ephiphany, but this is not really a nessessity. It would just be nice.[/QUOTE]
Lately I've come to think that Evince and Epiphany should merge - now that Poppler is that fast it isn't like loading Acrobat was anymore. Now it's just two document viewers with more in common than not, really. Several of the formats that Evince do have hyperlinks in them, and at least PDF gets even more common on the web all the time. And Evince could benefit from tabs, too. :)

So, let me add two more things:

* Possibility to tell the browser to open new Windows (target="_blank") in a new Tab, like FF can. This, if adopted by more browsers, would actually allow web designers to again design pages with target="_blank" and let end users decide which they like. Plus it's almost impossible to do without in day-to-day browsing for me. ;)

* Javascript based extensions. It makes just so much sense to code extensions in the language that is already built in (no python dependency if you don't want it), that has a good solid structure for DOM manipulation already in place, and is a standard that has worked very well for FF. Of course, they can also manipulate XUL via this, which makes *the UI, the tabs, and the HTML be part of the same DOM tree* - which is so awesome for developers it is almost unbelievable.

But apparently there's a faction saying "python is much easier", which is just point of view (and one that I disagree with personally). In either case, there's a reason there aren't many extensions available. It's hard to write them and support is patchy at best. Would love to work on say a web developers extension, but there just is no way to do that, except in C - and I'm not sure about that either.

There are many other things like that too. I'd really like to use Epiphany for various reasons, and has been a subscriber to the mailing list for like forever to keep up with news and give opinions. And it's quite possible that it is already a perfect browser for many people, but for someone who uses it as the main work tool (information, mail, wikis, webbased tools galore) it's not even close.

All in all, good work, keep it up, but there is a lot to do if I will ever use or recommend it daily.

---

### Post by Spark* on 2006-04-02
> **ago]Firefox

I have noticed that lots of new users move to linux/ubuntu after having experienced Firefox... Generally the story goes like this:

1) They try FireFox
2) They try OpenOffice/Gaim/Thunderbird...
3) They like what they see and they want to try the full monty

Now what happens when they come to ubuntu? 

No FF? No OO? 

Are we joking?[/quote]

Why should they be surprised that those applications don't come installed by default, when it's exactly the same on Windows? They might actually be amazed when they click on Add/Remove Applications, search for something they know (like "Firefox") and there it is, installable with one click! Now they know both Epiphany and Firefox and can choose whatever works best for them. If they actually like Epiphany, they are also less likely to go back to Windows soon.  said:**
> It does not integrate well with Gnome? Well maybe it is Gnome that should consider to achieve a better fit with FireFox and not the other way around. Particularly considering that 1) firefox is far more popular than gnome, 2) for many people the browser is the most used application, and interaction within the browser is far more important than interaction within the desktop

That's just ridiculous. The platform can't integrate with an application, that doesn't even remotely make sense. The platform provides the facilities which an application can either use or not use.

> 3) because the interface of firefox is better (see toolbar customization in firefox vs panel customeization in gnome)....

That is not true. Panel customization is a completely different use case from toolbar customization. Panel customization is much more about manipulating objects directly instead of going into a one-time edit mode. That's why applets have handles so you can move them around and you can move every object smoothly by dragging it with the middle mouse button (hold shift to push adjacent objects around).

If you tried the actual toolbar editing in Epiphany, then you know that it's exactly the same as in Firefox but even more polished. There are other cases in which the Epiphany UI simply works smoother, for example while moving tabs (which wasn't even possible in Firefox until not so long ago), they move instantly in Epiphany while you only get placeholders in Firefox. No close button on individual tabs isn't great as well, which they fortunately realized by now.

People make such a big deal out of this, just because some users spend most of their time browsing the web. But in the grand scale of things, it's still just another application. It wouldn't spell the end of Ubuntu if they'd ever switch to Epiphany by default and I bet you'd get more responses from people who are positively surprised than by people who have a practical (as opposed to religious) problem with it.

### Post by Anthem on 2006-04-04
Let it die, man, let it die.  Or at least do a search of the forum to see if a topic's already discussed.  Here's a recap.

It's not feasible in the near future, because Epiphany requires Firefox to be installed.  Check back again when XULrunner is working well.  Until then, it's either Epiph+Firefox or just Firefox.  Easy choice.

Ubuntu's Firefox is much slower than the stock firefox due to poor flag management during compilation.  If Firefox were compiled correctly, it would be just as fast as Epiphany.

We use best-of-breed apps.  Firefox is best-of-breed, Epiphany is not.  We use OOo Writer/OOo Calc instead of AbiWord/Gnumeric for the same reasons (although I personally prefer AbiWord).

Mark and several top developers have already weighed in, and all have said they're going to stay with Firefox for the forseeable future because it's an easier transition for most people moving from Windows.  So this whole thing is a tempest in a teapot.

If Firefox is too slow, try installing the stock one from mozilla.org.  Or file a bug report and try to get it fixed.  Or install Epiphany for yourself.  But don't push it on the rest of us, when the overwhelming majority see Firefox as a superior browser and as a better choice.

### Post by klahjn on 2006-04-04
I enjoy using firefox quite well.  Although epiphany does have some speed advantages over ff if you use the ubuntu build of firefox, those can be easily remedied by either adding one line of code, or building it from mozilla.org.  
add this line of code to /etc/environment        to increase your performance in Firefox:    MOZ_DISABLE_PANGO="1"
otherwise speaking, once that is done performance is approximately the same.  on top of that, i enjoy the added features FF brings to the table.  not only that, from the iface of epiphany, i kind of feel like it's a kids browser, but that may very well just be my opinion.  All the buttons are in the same place essentially, but it has that unpolished IE experience that i ever so don't enjoy.  Just my $0.02

### Post by Mathias-K on 2006-04-04
[QUOTE=linuxnomad]I hear the pro Firefox people arguing that Firefox should be kept as default for new Ubuntu users because its familar. But I am also hearing these same people talking about installing Firefox from source, or edit a config file because Ubuntu's build is slower. What new linux user, trying to come over from Windows, is going to want to do that.[/QUOTE]

I'm still a pretty new Linux user with Breezy being my first distro, so i can still pretty much remember what really mattered in talking me over to use Linux programs - because that was what i was afraid of. In the start i was very confused and all i saw was Gdraw, Kstartup, Xpicture and all kinds of what looked like cheap copies of Windows programs.

Here programs like Firefox stands out as something that is known to almost anyone who i'd think would be a real potential Linux user. 

[QUOTE=linuxnomad]Epiphany does everything Firefox can do for the basic user, and thats what we are talking about. (...)

What exactly can't you do with epiphany that you can do with firefox?[/QUOTE]

Do you know how many techies, nerds and power users who have some kind of Firefox wallpaper on their Windows XP desktops? I think it's a powerful argument to tell them that in Ubuntu, Firefox - "the best browser" - is of course the default one. 

I think popularizing Linux apps in Windows, like it could be happening with GIMP, Gaim and the like is very very important if Ubuntu is ever going to grow massively in tech circles.

### Post by ago on 2006-04-04
[QUOTE=Spark*]Why should they be surprised that those applications don't come installed by default, when it's exactly the same on Windows?[/quote]
Because that is what they are used to. And chances are that they moved to Linux exactly because they DO LIKE those applications, and most of their computing needs are covered. When in a new environment, finding applications you are familiar with is a GOOD thing. Particularly the ones that motivated the switch in the first place.

> They might actually be amazed when they click on Add/Remove Applications
Where they will find epiphany...

> In the other case, they would only know Firefox.
Hiding beloved applications in order to force people to discover the Add/Remove Apps section does not strike me as a "user friendly" approach...

> That's just ridiculous. The platform can't integrate with an application
But it can take ideas and implement some of the behaviour so that differences are reduced. And firefox is not strictly an application, it is also a platform. And a heck of a good one.

> That is not true. Panel customization is a completely different use case from toolbar customization. Panel customization is much more about manipulating objects directly instead of going into a one-time edit mode. That's why applets have handles so you can move them around and you can move every object smoothly by dragging it with the middle mouse button (hold shift to push adjacent objects around).
I for one do not see the difference between toolbar and panel at all. IMO the idea that the panel is seen as a piece of the desktop (that behaves like the desktop window) and not as a desktop toolbar is plain wrong.

I never felt the need to maniupulate objects on the panel all the time. Once I customize the panel I do not touch it for MONTHS. And this half-bred gnome interface is suboptimal both when I need to change the panel layout and when I don't want the panel to be changed. Which makes it completely suboptimal.

Toolbar customization in Firefox? A breeze. When you do lots of changes it is faster because you are always in edit mode and do not need to use menus or middleclick+shift or other similar "user friendly" combinations. And when you are done everything is locked for you, no confusing locks everywhere....

"Smooth object movement"??? The only use of having objects floating in the middle of my 1/2 inch panel I could think of is to irritate me... If I want a space, I'll use a spacer, thank you. But in 99.9% of the cases I want objects on the panel to be close to each other. It is much better to have discrete positioning of objects. Like... Firefox... You guessed it.

Then you need to add the fact that there are all those shortcut menu items which are 100% useless most of the times "add, remove, delete panel, move, edit, lock, bla, bla, bla".... Enter firefox... "customize", that's it... Talk to me about "user friendliness" and simple interface....

That said, I think energies would be much better used in trying to properly optimize the firefox builds to make them faster...

### Post by joshuapurcell on 2006-04-11
This is the thread that will never die, and only because there are some who won't recognize the fact that Firefox is going to be the default for quite some time (if not for any other reason than overwhelming popularity). And what is necessarily bad about that? It takes nothing away from those who believe Epiphany is better (since you can obviously install it through apt-get or we wouldn't be having this discussion), and Firefox also brings a nice dose of familiarity to a possibly unfamiliar OS for many people. That's not even going into the fact that Firefox has a much larger collection of extensions, themes, etc. than any other of the less popular browsers combined. If some of you want to use Epiphany, than use Epiphany and be happy in the knowledge that you are using a browser that fits you the best. At the same time you should recognize the benefits that the majority of users realize from using Firefox... both of these can happen without one group trying to change the other.

### Post by audax321 on 2006-04-11
I think out of Epiphany and Firefox, Firefox should be default. Epiphany is nice, but Firefox I think can be scaled from simple to advance much more easily using extensions.

Taking everything into account though, I think Opera is better than all three. It's quick, the new development releases hardly have any rendering issues, and NO MEMORY LEAKS. Although I have to admit that Opera's configuration is not as straight-forward as Firefox's. It has a lot of configuration options hidden inside menus and the UserJS script feature might be too much for a new user. So, I wouldn't recommend it as default. If it's all the same the user should be given a choice between a browser of their choice and Firefox without breaking the ubuntu-desktop package because there really is no point in having two browsers installed (e.g. have a ubuntu-desktop-nobrowser package and a ubuntu-desktop package with Firefox).

That way a new installation would install the ubuntu-desktop package by default and the end-user can optionally uninstall Firefox (thus breaking ubuntu-desktop) and selecting the ubuntu-desktop-nobrowser package for installation instead.

### Post by audax321 on 2006-04-12

The only thing they really have in common is the gecko engine. The idea, as was said earlier in this thread, is to just provide the gecko engine via a libgecko package. That way you wouldn't need to install the entire browser and Firefox/Epiphany would each just depend on the libgecko package.

### Post by Ambidistastrous on 2006-04-14
I thought the point of Dapper's delay was to make sure it's as polished as possible. At this point, Firefox is much more polished than Epiphany -- business-ready, in my opinion -- so it gets my vote for the moment.

I think the Opera option was a trick. ;)

But, some general arguments for Epiphany:

1) A web browser has a lot in common with a window manager, as far as the interface goes, so why not follow up with the integration? KDE did it with Konqueror. Someday,  Epiphany when works seamlessly with the rest of Gnome, it will make worlds o sense to set it as the default browser. Unfortunately, that day is not June 1, 2006, in my humble opinion.

2) As was the design philosophy with Firefox re Mozilla, it's a Good Thing to start with the most basic capabilities as the default, and add the power-user stuff as an optional extension. Epiphany is lighter-weight than Firefox, so there you go. Of course, if we're going that far, why not just use Dillo? ;)

3) F/OSS is about options, meaning it's NOT a Good Thing to try to impose Firefox as the sole, cross-platform ruler of the Web. Competition is what keeps Opera lean and mean, as a related example. There's no need to kill off Epi and Konqueror; some browsers should be designed to be universal, and some should be tailored to a particular desktop environment.

4) Yes, let's spin off a libgecko! I have no idea how feasible that is, but it sounds like a great idea.

---

### Post by Fluffy, the jolly sheep on 2006-11-23
I am kind of neutral about it, as ff 2 has taken over some good things of epiphany. I keep changing between epiphany and firefox.

My take on epiphany:

+: 
[LIST]
[*]nice animations when tab dragging, altering tool bar
[*]bookmark system with tags
[*]all gtk, gnome-integrated
[*]easier dialog to choose between downloading/opening a file
[*]download window has icon in notification area
[*]searching from within address bar
[/LIST]

-: 
[LIST]
[*]extensions: less available, no 3rd party extensions
[*]mouse gestures with middle mouse button
[*]some annoying unresolved bugs: no search anymore in address bar in edgy version and when using search function, page sometimes jumps
[*]horrible bookmark manager, should support context menu within bookmark menu
[*]also hate the history window, should be in a sidebar, also love ff history menu
[*]should support easy adding of search engines like firefox 
[*]when opening files like pdfs, seems to save them in my home directory but should just be temporary files
[/LIST]

hardyn: it's safe to remove evolution, but before you do an upgrade of ubuntu in the future, best to install the ubuntu-desktop package again (and thereby re-installing evolution)

### Post by aysiu on 2006-11-24
I've merged this with the previous discussion we had about this for Dapper, since the arguments remain essentially unchanged, and we *did* have a poll attached to that one.

I can sum up the main arguments for you, though, despite that fact that people will continue to rehash them:

**Epiphany**
Faster, lighter-weight
Official Gnome browser, better integration

**Firefox**
Familiarity for ex-Windows users who probably switched because of Firefox
Most people use Firefox anyway, so it's one less thing for them to install

There are other arguments, of course, for both sides, but those are the main ones.

### Post by kiddo on 2006-11-24
Time for some counter-argumentation :) my replies on this thread date back to very long ago.

> So why this discussion? Since Firefox is far more intuitive, pretty and functional than epiphany?IMHO, it's exactly the opposite. Do NOT ask any of my aunts to understand firefox; they will be MUCH more comfortable with Epiphany. I am speaking out of experience here (more than 5 people, didn't count them)

> Epiphany may be faster than Firefox, but isn't linux version of firefox faster than the windows version of Internet Explorer?
Nope, certainly not. Firefox is dog slow. Really. It's worse than in Windows if I recall correctly, and even in Windows, Firefox is usually pretty much slower than IE and eats more power. The reasons are of course that IE is integrated (and always loaded) in Windows, and, furthermore, mozilla hackers are a LOT more concerned about the Windows version. It gets a lot more attention than the Linux version. Why do you think we still don't have native GTK form widgets after all those years? Because they are not focused on Linux enough (if I'm not mistaken). See [http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=315932](http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=315932) and [https://bugzilla.mozilla.org/show_bug.cgi?id=232553](https://bugzilla.mozilla.org/show_bug.cgi?id=232553)

My point is: I think Firefox on Linux is either equal or worse than the Windows version, but I did not do benchmarks myself (and I don't have the skills for that).

But even if Epiphany is a tiny bit faster, the "firefox is still faster than in windows" argument does not stand; I mean, if epiphany is  even faster than something that is faster than firefox faster than IE, then why not just choose the best then? That means epiphany.

> now, refresh my memory: what is keeping epiphany from being installed without also having firefox installed? is it the gecko engine?Actually, the reason is a lot more stupid than that. Epiphany can be built against XULRunner (or whatever it is called) since MONTHS. The reason why it is not in ubuntu is because developers don't want to maintain Epiphany & Firefox at the same time (at least that's what I understood from the reply I got when I filed a bug: [https://launchpad.net/distros/ubuntu/+source/epiphany-browser/+bug/65231](https://launchpad.net/distros/ubuntu/+source/epiphany-browser/+bug/65231))

Arrrrgh. To think that on debian you can have it without a dependency on Firefox makes me mad. I don't want to go to debian >_<

> How about the ability to use plugins to play streaming video? This is such an aintegral part of the web. I had it working in opera for a few weeks then it stopped. In Firefox embedded videos work on most sites.Your question is too generic, what videos exactly? I can play apple trailers, a few other embedded videos, and all Flash videos. Epiphany is exactly the same as Firefox for video support, as far as I know.

> Besides, I could not find a search function in Epiphany, which I use all the time with Firefox, a symbiotic relationship with the tab functions (search, pop open a new window..rinse and repeat.)Do you mean searching google or searching as you type? If you want to search as you type, just ctrl-F or use the big "search" button.
If you want to google, just type what you want to search in the addressbar and select "Search the blahblahblah" from the dropdown menu that appears (normally, you could just press enter, but a stupid, **very** annoying bug in Edgy Eft prevents me from doing that and I need to use the dropdown menu).

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2006-12-19
Epiphany runs Javascript such as that of Slashdot or used by Xinha on an ancient 500 mHz Celeron at least 3 times faster than Firefox on a 1.8 gHz Athlon.
Enough said.
(Not enough for me, though. Fear the worst; I have 6 hours here!)
It could actually be a problem specific to my computer, though, since it runs fine elsewhere *

I'm not saying Firefox is a bad browser, but I think Epiphany is more suited to being the default in Ubuntu.
Accessibility is an important thing in any operating system, and Epiphany is more accessible because it runs well on old, slow, clunky machines. Ubuntu has great support for old hardware and it should stay that way. Firefox can be dog slow; it's a fancy sort of browser for people that like fancy web browsers.
It often takes up lots RAM, thus making it very difficult for people like me to use a web browser if I am running the live CD as a rescue disk on the bad computer.
Epiphany right now (5 tabs, some Javascipt, lots of Extensions) is taking 25.3 MB of RAM and that number is not changing by the second.

Epiphany also integrates well with the desktop, which is *vital* for any default applications!
It is insane to have an application in the default install which uses its own updater; there is a perfectly good updater already that the base install especially should be encouraging the use of. Including a web browser which doesn't use it is absurd.
Besides the updater stuff, Epiphany is actually "Gnome web browser". That means it is *built for Gnome.* It uses Gnome UI components as much as possible (as opposed to Firefox, which seems to use red, Windows-esque close buttons on tabs). If it was given a bit of love, that Gnome foot could be replaced with a nice Ubuntu logo thus giving it the final touch of integration.
Since it is built for Gnome, Epiphany runs best in Gnome; it is not burdened with hacks to cater to the wishes of every platform on the face of the Earth.

Epiphany is also pretty featureful when it comes to viewing content on the Web. Be sure to get its big extension pack and try a few of them; they add a bit of meat to it :)
There is a spell checker (sometimes...), and in terms of asthetics, moving tabs about is nicely done. Instead of a strange purpley arrow popping up, the tabs smoothly roll into place as I drag one around. It's not really an important thing, but it looks cool.

Don't get me wrong, Firefox is a neat browser. I'm running the FF3 trunk build on my better computer, and it also has FF2 as the default browser (in Windows). FF2 is even installed on this horribly slow one, because there are some neat extensions like Google Browser Sync which I like to use from time to time.

People were really bothered by how The GIMP ended up with an out-of-place splash screen that didn't look in the slightest bit like Ubuntu. And yet, we have  a whole web browser that doesn't quite fit in and it's hardly an issue!
(Personally I think neither are a *big issue*, but consistency is good and it would be nice...)


Edit:
Wait a minute... why am I bumping this?!
How did I get here?
Oops... *Sneaks away*

---

### Post by Anthem on 2006-12-26
Man, I thought this thread had been dead for ages.

I continue to think the GNOME integration card gets overplayed.  There's no stop to it... now Firefox, next OpenOffice, and what then?  It makes a ton of sense to have a suite of common programs that are the same in Ubuntu/Kubuntu/Xubuntu, especially ones that can be run on Windows as well.  If you've ever migrated somebody from Windows to Linux, you know it's a big deal for them to be able to switch to Free applications on Windows first, then to a Free operating system.

Ironically, I find AbiWord/Gnumeric to be worlds better than OOo Writer/Calc.  But at least it's cross-platform.



EDIT:  I really believe that if Ubuntu's Firefox builds weren't so bad, this conversation would have died a long time ago.

### Post by Anthem on 2006-12-26
Follow-up:

Now if somebody were to make Epiphany/Evolution/AbiWord/Gnumeric installable all at once, and cross-platform, and with an integrated look/feel, and able to seamlessly talk to each other, then we'd be in business.  But until then, Epiphany isn't a good choice for the default.

Part of the reason I don't like the "integrated" comments is that besides following a common HIG, these applications aren't integrated.  They don't feel similar when you use them, they don't look the same, they don't act the same.  OpenOffice and Firefox look no more out of place than anything else.

### Post by wipet on 2006-12-26
> **Anthem said:**
> Follow-up:

Now if somebody were to make Epiphany/Evolution/AbiWord/Gnumeric installable all at once, and cross-platform, and with an integrated look/feel, and able to seamlessly talk to each other, then we'd be in business.  But until then, Epiphany isn't a good choice for the default.

Part of the reason I don't like the "integrated" comments is that besides following a common HIG, these applications aren't integrated.  They don't feel similar when you use them, they don't look the same, they don't act the same.  OpenOffice and Firefox look no more out of place than anything else.

I think you are wrong here.
Epiphany use your GTK theme to the letter, Firefox does not (lot of custom widgets)
Epiphany follow global Gnome settings (like toolbars settings), Firefox does not.
Epiphany follow your Gnome icons theme, Firefox does not.
Firefox shortcuts are not consistant with the rest of the Gnome desktop (look at the menu shortcuts, at least in french they are very different, for exemple).
Epiphany use other Gnome applications, like Yelp or the GTK print dialog, Firefox does not.
Epiphany look and feel is much more consistant with the rest of the Gnome desktop. I am not saying it's a better application, it just feel more "gnomish" than Firefox. The same arguments can be said for Abiword, Gnumeric or Evolution (even if this one is not very good :P).

PS: sorry for my english, I am not a very good english writer, especially the day after Christmas

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2006-12-28
I just got a most interesting error message from Epiphany:> Firefox doesn't know how to open this address, because the protocol (define) isn't associated with any program.Therefore, since Epiphany thinks it is Firefox anyway, FF users should feel right at home :b

Actually, I've also realized that I don't really mind. If everything went my way, we would all be using Links in graphics mode by default (which I am using more and more often, when I need to browse the web quickly and have the whole system consumed by something else). Maybe it's a good thing that my way isn't the default.

Two web browsers at once from the start seems really clumsy and as though the OS has no direction.
It would be cool if we had Epiphany by default, but I can see why people prefer Firefox since it is the more popular, more stylish web browser which lots of people used to other operating systems will recognize. Firefox also has the bonus of stellar documentation, a great web site and at least an attempt to get people clued in to the existence of tabs.
I also think the Epiphany default layout needs a massive makeover (I have it looking really nice over here just by changing the toolbars, and I forgot how ugly / daunting it looks at first).

Of course, it can't be said that one is more user-friendly than the other. That above fool who doesn't yet notice the tabs bar was using Epiphany just like any other web browser the moment I explained "Epiphany is a web browser" :P

Some day down the road, maybe we will have a 'smart' installer which chooses defaults based on system specs. Until then, I will try to find what I can do to help rather than needlessly debate which should be the better off some day down the road.

### Post by hanzomon4 on 2006-12-28
After reading this thread I started testing Epiphany, but I have a question. I know how to add a live bookmark  , say google search, but I want to know if I can [list=1][*]Add an icon instead of the bookmark name(like the firefox search box)[*]Toogle between different live bookmarks(Like you can do with the firefox searchbox)[/list]

### Post by kiddo on 2006-12-28
> **hanzomon4 said:**
> I want to know if I can [list=1][*]Add an icon instead of the bookmark name(like the firefox search box)[*]Toogle between different live bookmarks(Like you can do with the firefox searchbox)[/list](...) Also how do I stop Epiphany from opening new windows and use tabs instead?

Hello Hanzomon, congratulations for your will to try out new stuff :) let me ask you some questions that may answer yours:
- epiphany allows using smart bookmarks right from the location bar. Normally, if you start typing something where the page URL is, you can see a dropdown menu with your bookmarks, history, and smart bookmarks. Selecting one of those smart bookmarks would do the search for you. Wouldn't that be better, or you still want the firefox dropdown search field (which I think is useless when you can have it all in the location bar, imho)?

Opening tabs by default: go in your gnome menu: System > Preferences > Preferred applications or favorite applications or whatever it is called in English (you can also run "gnome-default-applications-properties" to access that preferences dialog).
In that dialog, you will easily be able to set epiphany as the default browser and set it to open everything in new tabs. Hope that helps.

### Post by kiddo on 2006-12-31
Yeah Epiphany was actually initiated by one of the (or THE?) initiators of Galeon, who felt that he wanted a smaller and more HIG-compliant browser. Since Epiphany as pretty much caught on galeon's features, there is not much reason for Galeon to exist anymore. 

> would someone tell me why is it (in Epiphany) impossible to configure the mouse gesture button to anything else than middle button?

[http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=320525](http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=320525)

I just read the bug report, and saw this comment, which pretty much explains a lot of things, and makes some sense from my point of view:
> Context menus should open on mouse*down*, not on mouse*up*. This allows for "single click" usage of menus:
1. press right button,
2. move mouse (trackball in my case) to menu,
3. release right button
This is how all menus in Gnome work and I would really hate to see this changing. (In fact, this is one of my main annoyances when I'm stuck on a windows machine at my university).

On the other hand,
> [...]a laptop. Ever tried these crazy left-right-point actions? If people have to use 3 fingers to actually use a function which should make surfing the web easier, I really don't get the point ... to which it was replied that > Gestures with pointing devices other than mice or trackballs are not really useful, are they? Gestures are hand movements, not fingertip rolling...

My take: if anyone feels like hacking a little bit and submitting a patch, I'm pretty certain they will accept it.

---

### Post by gnomeuser on 2007-01-01
> **detyabozhye said:**
> Ah, sorry. But I did have one real question I forgot to ask:

Is there a good GTK+ browser somewhat like Epiphany that doesn't depend on gnome libs besides Firefox?
And Epiphany was a fork of Galeon, right? What's the deal they had about merging the browsers again or something like that?

Galeon was the roots of Epiphany, Epiphany branched off with a different focus and was quickly elected to be the official webbrowser for the GNOME desktop. Galeon development and use thus faded quite a bit and recently it was announced that Galeon would shift it's focus from developing their entirely own browser to simply creating plugins for Epiphany. Less work for the same result and hopefully wider use, seems like a good deal to me and I hope this leads to fruitful cooperation.

---

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2007-01-28
Sorry to bump this, but I decided that building my own thread would be an even more horrible act than what I have now done.
In my searching for what to do with that weird Sidebar extension in Epiphany, I discovered this excellent article:
[http://ploum.frimouvy.org/?2006/03/15/100-why-you-should-try-epiphany-as-your-default-browser-with-gnome-214](http://ploum.frimouvy.org/?2006/03/15/100-why-you-should-try-epiphany-as-your-default-browser-with-gnome-214)

It has done two things for me:

-My appreciation of Epiphany as a great web browser has been reinforced.

-I have become aware of the better end of Gnome's simplicity, which is where programs do one single thing well (instead of doing 10 things badly). I suppose, for the most part, this does happen in Gnome and it is certainly how I like Linux to be :)

There is also this article:
[http://blog.codechunk.net/2006/09/26/making-epiphany-usable/](http://blog.codechunk.net/2006/09/26/making-epiphany-usable/)

I also discovered just how easy it is to build extensions for Epiphany, thanks to Python!
Working on my dream bookmarking tool right now... Considering a daring glance at the source code; it may suit my exacting standards (or my lack of understanding thereof), thus being suitable for me 
to mess with. Who knows? Maybe my insistence that people should contribute more to Epiphany's growth won't be so hypocritical after all...

I *do* wish Epiphany had more interesting tab support. (Dragging tabs between windows, etc...)
It's also a bit of a shame that the tabs bar has such a horrible way of treating overflows. Tabs disappear off the side and arrows appear, but the only way to get to the tabs out the sides is to flip through every single tab along with way! (You can't just scroll the tabs bar). Apparently a problem with GTK, so presumably when they decide to do something about it we will see a wonderful improvement in Epiphany at the same time. (Ah, the wonders of desktop-integrated software).
(Oh, and the spell checker is weird in that it only sometimes operates, and it doesn't give suggestions :( )

Anyway, this is my last useless "contribution" to this poor, unending thread. I promise!

---

### Post by Rab22 on 2007-01-28
[B]My Thoughts....
[/B]

Epiphany is a very nice browser, don't get me wrong. It is true that it incorporates very nicely within GNOME -- and I'm all for GNOME application consistency. 

The biggest issue I have with Epiphany is it's idea of bookmark management. I really don't see that being a feature that the common user would understand. You label a bookmark as several different 'tags' and then it only goes under one? With countless amounts of websites now using tags/labels -- this should be reconsidered.

However, that is not the biggest resson I feel that Firefox should be the default. A year ago, I'd fully agreed with you that Epiphany should be the default. In fact, I think I voted for it. However, look at how well Firefox now feels like a GNOME application. Yet, still offers those features that users from Windows would be accustomed to. 

Maybe a good idea would be to have both browsers instaleld by default -- and allow the user to switch between them by use of a 'setup wizard'. In fact, I think a setup wizard that sets up the default browser, e-mail client, quickly adds a username (or others) to Gaim if selected, and customizes some GNOME settings would be nice.

---

### Post by kiddo on 2007-01-28
> **Rab22 said:**
> You label a bookmark as several different 'tags' and then it only goes under one? With countless amounts of websites now using tags/labels -- this should be reconsidered.Could you explain that/point me to a bug report? If I bookmark this page under tags foo, bar, it will appear under both topics. I can't see what you mean?

> A year ago, I'd fully agreed with you that Epiphany should be the default. [...] However, look at how well Firefox now feels like a GNOME application. Yet, still offers those features that users from Windows would be accustomed to.
Hmm... I'm not sure I agree: I have not seen the Firefox interface integrate better from 1.0 to 2.0. What has progressed so much since last year, could provide examples? Here is what I still see:
[LIST]
[*]toolbars do not use the current icon theme - **providing a Tango icon theme is *not* a solution**, it's working around the problem!
[*]**toolbars items do not follow the "style"** of the environment. For example, they display items as tiny icons no matter what. I have my desktop set to "large icons with text under them", but I could also set it to "text besides {important} icons" or "text only"
[*]the download Firefox **download manager is a joke** compared to epiphany, from a usability standpoint. Discovering the one in epiphany was simply a complete fountain of joy for me, honestly
[*]the **tabs look out of place** and **react a bit slowly** on Firefox (the ones in epiphany are limited to the speed of your gtk engine/theme only); furthermore, dragging them is not what I would call "**direct manipulation**" (if I compare to epiphany of course)
[*]there are **no icons besides Firefox's menu items**
[*]Firefox's **loading "spinner"** is in a weird place (breaks consistency with nautilus, for example)
[*]I just realized while toying with Firefox that the menus are not **"real" menus**. This will become apparent if you use Compiz (or beryl) with the Trailfocus plugin. If you open a menu in Epiphany, it shows normally; if you open a menu in Firefox, it appears transparent or greyed out (depending on your trailfocus settings) - proof that it is not considered a "real" menu by the window manager
[/LIST]

I'm not saying Firefox is crap, I'm just pointing out some "desktop integration issues" that I listed over 10 minutes of checking. And I really, really doubt that Mozilla Corp will spend work force into fixing those further; we don't even have native form widgets yet :|

Edit: by the way, just to add some fuel to the debate, I tried out Epiphany 2.17.x in Feisty, and the **adblock** plugin finally has a very comprenhensive configuration UI. It even has a filterset.g updater built-in, you only have to click one button. This is great.

### Post by CarpKing on 2007-01-29
Epiphany is a nice browser, and integration is a good thing, but I still prefer Firefox.  I'm sure if I really dug into it I could get most things in Epiphany working more like Firefox, but there are a few things I doubt will do so.  For one, I use live bookmarks (RSS feeds) a lot, and Epiphany makes a point of using an external reader.  Perhaps I misunderstand what RSS feeds are supposed to do, but I use them to quickly open new articles in the browser.  I also make use of the separate search field.  

Epiphany does have some good points, though.  It feels a bit lighter and seems to take less memory.  Autoscrolling, while it flickers when it goes too fast, doesn't slow down as much as Firefox when I use Beryl (the latter is a video card driver issue, and the former may be as well). I suspect the sidebar extension may allow one to open the history there instead of in a separate window, but I saw no easy way to do this.  It would also be nice (for both Epiphany and Firefox) if the extensions dialog was somehow integrated into the preferences dialog.  

I read on the Gnome bugtacker about Epiphany showing previews in the filechooser, which would be an awesome feature.  The lack of this ability in Firefox in Linux has vexed me greatly, and I would probably use Epiphany much of the time if it had this (I often fire up Galeon just to use this feature, though Galeon has more things that annoy me than Epiphany does).  Sadly, the discussion [here]("http://bugzilla.gnome.org/show_bug.cgi?id=144337") is quite old and it doesn't seem to have yet come to fruition.
