### Post by yigal.weinstein on 2007-04-10
1. epiphany is faster than firefox on a slower system
2. epiphany uses an innovative Bookmarking system.
3. epiphany is meant to go along with the GNOME desktop the desktop of Ubuntu
4.  This is why gnome thinks its better and I agree with them,

> Epiphany's GNOME integration is better than Firefox's. This includes:

    * Simplicity - Epiphany aims for the simplest interface possible for a browser. Simplicity can be achieved by providing advanced features in an intuitive manner. Epiphany's modularity helps make a light and powerful application. External applications and components are used when possible. Examples: 'View source' launches a gedit window instead of a browser view source window, and 'Help' launches Yelp instead of a foreign help browser.
    *

      HIG compliance - Epiphany aims to follow GNOME Human Interface Guidelines (HIG) version 2.0. Non-compliance with HIG 2.0 is a bug, unless there are grave reasons to make an exception. Contrast with Firefox, which follows a few HIG recommendations (button order, and Preferences under Edit instead of Tools), while its dialogs use the Cancel/OK idiom.
    *

      Localisation consistency - Epiphany translations are managed by the GNOME translation project. This ensures a much greater consistency level with other translated GNOME components than Firefox can give, where translations must also blend in with Windows. Furthermore, some Mozilla-provided translations are low quality, and Mozilla translations are always beyond the control of the GTP.
    * Complete lockdown by gconf keys or with Pessulus - indispensible for system administrators or kiosk mode.
    * Follows the icon theme - because Epiphany uses the GNOME framework, it will instantly use the icon theme you've selected. Firefox equivalents have been painstakingly recreated for some themes, but people who are using one which lacks an equivalent, are out of luck.
    * Time-based releases - Since Epiphany's release cycle is synchronized with GNOME's, Epiphany quickly benefits from improvements in the GNOME platform, such as D-BUS and new GTK releases.
    * Support for GNOME accessibility features such as themes for users with vision disabilities
    *

      Integration with the NetworkManager applet ensures that Epiphany automatically knows when the network is unreachable and switches to offline mode.
[http://live.gnome.org/Epiphany/ProjectFAQ](http://live.gnome.org/Epiphany/ProjectFAQ)

CONS

### Post by Darkhack on 2007-09-05
> **tehkain said:**
> Once the webkit/gtk project is complete I am sure epiphany will gain alot of momentum.

I'm currently a Firefox user but I am *really* looking forward to seeing Epiphany with Webkit and as people development more extensions, seeing it evolve into a great browser.

> **triptoe said:**
> My biggest gripe with Firefox is the XUL interface.

For me it is the combination of XUL and Gecko.  Both are garbage.  If you think about it, Firefox was doomed to failure from the beginning by choosing to build a "light-weight browser" on top of such bloated libraries.

> **triptoe said:**
> What exactly is webkit? Is it a replacement for gecko?

Basically.  [Webkit]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Webkit") is just another [rendering engine]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rendering_engine") that was originally based off of [KHTML]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KHTML").  Apple decided to fork KHTML into Webkit for their Safari browser and recently the two have merged so that now KDE will be using Webkit instead of backporting Apple's patches into KHTML. The backporting proved to be a very difficult process because of the major changes Apple made when they forked it.  Now that they are combined though, development should greatly improve.  The main reason people like Webkit over [Gecko]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gecko_%28layout_engine%29") is two-fold.  First, it is actually more standards compliant than Gecko, and second, performance tests have shown that it is up to two times faster.  Now combine this with a truly light-weight browser like Epiphany that uses a native toolkit instead of XUL, and you have one hell of a browser.  Trust me, I'm drooling over it and everyone else should too.  You'll be floored when you see the performance difference.

> **daveshields said:**
> Firefox is the standard. That's what most folks would expect, and it's what they are getting now. There's no reason to change.

There isn't reason to change yet, but as more people develop Epiphany extensions and Webkit has better integration with Epiphany, you'll be hard to find reasons not to change.

---

like here:

[http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8gh7ABSs1NY](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8gh7ABSs1NY)

Doesn't happen to my windows. Course I am using Beryl (doesn't happen on Compiz either, no spikes), all my windows draw awesome :). I might add my machine is 5+ years old now. I switched to Metacity and did notice this, seems to be an issue between Metacity and XUL.

As for being default, my answer is keep Firefox. The majority of people here and in the Windows world like and use it, only makes sense to keep it default. Epiphany is nice (I've tried it) but I (like others) need my favourite extensions.

Apple decided to fork KHTML into Webkit for their Safari browser and recently the two have merged so that now KDE will be using Webkit instead of backporting Apple's patches into KHTML. The backporting proved to be a very difficult process because of the major changes Apple made when they forked it.  Now that they are combined though, development should greatly improve. ### Post by tehkain on 2007-09-05
I compiled epiphany with webkit today and I hope to be able to package it for getdeb in the future when it becomes more stable. It is fast and as always GTK makes XUL lame.

Everyone check out:
[http://www.atoker.com/blog/2007/09/05/webkitgtk-at-linuxconf-europe-2007/](http://www.atoker.com/blog/2007/09/05/webkitgtk-at-linuxconf-europe-2007/)

PS, for those who are wondering if there is anyway to get epiphany to have a search entry box like firefox PM me and I will message you in a few days when I get my launchpad project set up. It was very easy to add a search bar with the ability to add engines with the epiphany plugin system. It took some 50 lines of code to have a fully expandable, uses gconf keys manually but I am planning on menu interface to add new engines tonite, gtk search bar. Writting plugins in python with the epiphany API is as easy as pie. If anyone has any other suggestions for epiphany plugins let me know.

### Post by mthei on 2007-09-05
I don't mind that Firefox comes as the default in Ubuntu. What gets me is that you don't have the option to remove it in favour of Epiphany (or whatever browser the user prefers). As has been mentioned, it's not that hard to install new software, and for those who are coming from a different OS like Windows or Mac, they should obviously be expecting a bit of a learning curve. But yeah, I'd like to see something other than Firefox as a the default. My current default browser is Opera, which I love but that obviously can't come pre-installed, and I know a lot of people prefer open source apps (usually I do as well, with Opera being the one exception), so it's nice to see good alternatives. I'm curious to see Epiphany with a new rendering engine, as I always liked its performance, even with Gecko.

Edit: I should say, however, that I still do use Firefox from time to time, and I've never experienced extreme instability or memory usage. Swiftfox and Swiftweasel on the other hand have peaked well over the 100mb of RAM mark. Firefox usually hovers around the 60mb point at its highest.

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2007-09-25
Epiphany is awesome. Its bookmarks, in particular. I am really looking forward to a Summer of Code project which connects Bookmarks and History, as it will make navigating really easy. I can already get to a bookmark by typing a part of it in the address bar, so navigating History the same way would be fantastic!

Those who dislike Epiphany clearly haven't experienced its natural bookmark tagging feature.
They also have clearly not experienced the real joys of desktop-integrated applications (when they are integrated with a good desktop like GNOME), which is probably largely to do with the serious lack of them in Ubuntu's default install.

Epiphany also has reasonably tidy code, and it isn't as complex a beast as Firefox, so it seems a lot nicer to make little changes to.

### Post by RAOF on 2007-09-27
> **glosman15 said:**
> ...It's annoying that to use Epiphany I need to have Firefox installed, just sitting there sucking up 30 MB of space doing absolutely nothing at all....

Technically it's not doing nothing at all.  It's actually providing Epiphany's rendering engine, since we don't separate firefox's rendering engine from it's front end (because upstream firefox doesn't yet).  The other rendering engines Epiphany can use (gtkhtml, WebKit) don't work as well as gecko, although getting WebKit working shouldn't take toooooo long.  Maybe for Hardy or Hardy+1.

---

### Post by Mr. Picklesworth on 2007-09-27
FIrefox with a detached back-end (or Epiphany using a well-engineered rendering engine that isn't tethered to a specific browser) will be a great day indeed. I hate having messages in Epiphany that are Firefox-specific. Things like RSS feeds do this, I have had Firefox's download manager appear on me once, and some kinds of errors / warnings do this.

I disagree with the notion that Firefox has power in the mainstream and thus should be kept as installed by default. This is an entire operating system here; 'having Firefox' is not going to magically make people comfortable learning the thing. Actually, since Firefox does not behave like the rest of the desktop, it would make them feel *less* comfortable.
To be fair, OpenOffice is actually significantly worse than Firefox at desktop integration (since it also has one of the most irritating Options screens ever -- quite in line with Microsoft Office), but at least it has a legitimate reason to exist, which is that we need a good office suite and OpenOffice is it. Firefox is just a web browser, though, and we already have a lot of web browsers here that actually do fit *perfectly* with the desktop.

The web browser is one of the most frequently used components of a modern computer desktop. People learn the advanced features available on GNOME's desktop by using programs that fit on it, and Firefox definitely does not fit. People cannot learn Ubuntu by using Firefox, whereas using a browser like Epiphany, one is continually able to learn how this desktop environment behaves differently, and everything he then expects to happen from using other programs on the desktop *does* happen in Epiphany (but not in Firefox).

GNOME (and by extension, Ubuntu) shows its real power when every program uses the correct libraries for user interface. In particular, when every program accepts the desktop-wide defaults for toolbars, when every program uses the stock icons defined in the current theme, and when every program has smoothly resizable widgets using fonts set by the user. Firefox does none of these, and there are a lot of other things it fails to do, such as configurable menu accelerators.

Bottom line: Firefox behaves a lot like a Windows program, because Windows is its target audience. The only thing Firefox's presence does for Ubuntu is say "look, we're like Windows!" (which is absolutely not the truth, and is not going to convince anyone who sees beyond Firefox to the very different desktop). It further bludgeons this claim - which should be a complete untruth - into reality by the fact that people are using it *all the time*.

---

### Post by Quillz on 2007-09-27
I think Firefox makes a better default browser and should remain as such, but I could understand why some would want Epiphany to the default. It's the "official" GNOME browser, and as such integrates into GNOME much better. Not to mention that Epiphany is both simple yet functional, like Safari on OS X. And that's what the default browser should be. Something that loads the Internet and then gets out of your way.
